<td><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>

<tr>
<td>Appearance
</td>
<td>White crystalline solid, almost colorless liquid with a hint of blue, colorless gas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun_1993_612_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braun_1993_612-3">[3]</a></sup>
</td></tr>

</td>
<td>Liquid:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiddick1970Table_of_Physical_Properties,_Water_0b._pg_67-8_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiddick1970Table_of_Physical_Properties,_Water_0b._pg_67-8-4">[4]</a></sup><br/>0.9998396 g/mL at 0 °C<br/>0.9970474 g/mL at 25 °C<br/>0.961893 g/mL at 95 °C<br/>Solid:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide2003Properties_of_Ice_and_Supercooled_Water_in_Section_6_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide2003Properties_of_Ice_and_Supercooled_Water_in_Section_6-5">[5]</a></sup><br/> 0.9167 g/ml at 0 °C
</td></tr>
<tr>

<td> 0.00 °C (32.00 °F; 273.15 K) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VSMOW_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-VSMOW-7">[a]</a></sup>

Miscible with <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propanol" title="Propanol">propanol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isopropanol" title="Isopropanol">isopropanol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1,4-dioxane" title="1,4-dioxane">1,4-dioxane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrofuran" title="Tetrahydrofuran">tetrahydrofuran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfolane" title="Sulfolane">sulfolane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimethylformamide" title="Dimethylformamide">dimethylformamide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimethoxyethane" title="Dimethoxyethane">dimethoxyethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_sulfoxide" title="Dimethyl sulfoxide">dimethyl sulfoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetonitrile" title="Acetonitrile">acetonitrile</a>. Partially miscible with <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">Diethyl ether</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methyl_Ethyl_Ketone" title="Methyl Ethyl Ketone">Methyl Ethyl Ketone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dichloromethane" title="Dichloromethane">Dichloromethane</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethyl_Acetate" title="Ethyl Acetate">Ethyl Acetate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">Bromine</a>.

<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;">
</td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Water</b> (<span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polar</a> <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compound</a> that is at <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a> a tasteless and odorless <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, which is nearly colorless apart from <a href="/wiki/Color_of_water" title="Color of water">an inherent hint of blue</a>. It is by far the most studied chemical compound and is described as the "universal <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>" <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997620_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997620-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> and the "solvent of life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201348_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201348-22">[20]</a></sup> It is the most abundant substance on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20162_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20162-23">[21]</a></sup> and the only common substance to exist as a <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">solid</a>, liquid, and <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">gas</a> on Earth's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201344_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201344-24">[22]</a></sup> It is also the third most abundant molecule in the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20162_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20162-23">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water molecules form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bonds" title="Hydrogen bonds">hydrogen bonds</a> with each other and are strongly polar. This polarity allows it to separate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">ions</a> in salts and strongly bond to other polar substances such as alcohols and acids, thus dissolving them. Its hydrogen bonding causes its many unique properties, such as having a solid form less dense than its liquid form, a relatively high <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> of 100 °C for its <a href="/wiki/Molar_mass" title="Molar mass">molar mass</a>, and a high <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">heat capacity</a>.
</p><p>Water is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoteric" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a>, meaning that it exhibits properties of both an acid and a base; it readily produces both <a href="/wiki/Hydron_(chemistry)" title="Hydron (chemistry)"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H+_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-H+-25">[c]</a></sup> Related to its amphoteric character, it undergoes <a href="/wiki/Self-ionization_of_water" title="Self-ionization of water">self-ionization</a>. The product of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_activity" title="Chemical activity">activities</a>, or approximately, the concentrations of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is a constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_chemistry_analysis" title="Water chemistry analysis">Water chemistry analysis</a></div>
<p>Water is the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">chemical substance</a> with <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>; one <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a> of water has two <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent" title="Covalent">covalently</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">bonded</a> to a single <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> atom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbellWilliamsonHeyden2006_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbellWilliamsonHeyden2006-27">[24]</a></sup>
Water is a tasteless, odorless liquid at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_conditions" title="Standard conditions">ambient temperature and pressure</a>, and appears colorless in small quantities, although it has its own intrinsic very light blue hue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun_1993_612_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Braun_1993_612-3">[3]</a></sup> Ice also appears colorless, and water vapor is essentially invisible as a gas.
</p><p>Unlike other analogous hydrides of the <a href="/wiki/Chalcogen" title="Chalcogen">oxygen family</a>, water is primarily a liquid under <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard conditions</a> due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bonding" title="Hydrogen bonding">hydrogen bonding</a>. The molecules of water are constantly moving in relation to each other, and the hydrogen bonds are continually breaking and reforming at timescales faster than 200 femtoseconds (2×10<sup>−13</sup> seconds).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup>
However, these bonds are strong enough to create many of the peculiar properties of water, some of which make it integral to life.
</p>
<h3><span id="Water.2C_ice.2C_and_vapor"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Water,_ice,_and_vapor">Water, ice, and vapor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Water, ice, and vapor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Within the Earth's atmosphere and surface, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_phase" title="Liquid phase">liquid phase</a> is the most common and is the form that is generally denoted by the word "water". The <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid phase</a> of water is known as <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a> and commonly takes the structure of hard, amalgamated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystals" title="Crystals">crystals</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_cubes" title="Ice cubes">ice cubes</a>, or loosely accumulated <a href="/wiki/Granular_material" title="Granular material">granular</a> crystals, like <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a>. Aside from <a href="/wiki/Ice_Ih" title="Ice Ih">common hexagonal crystalline ice</a>, other crystalline and amorphous <a href="/wiki/Ice#Phases" title="Ice">phases of ice</a> are known. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaseous_phase" title="Gaseous phase">gaseous phase</a> of water is known as <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a>). Visible steam and clouds are formed from minute droplets of water suspended in the air.
</p><p>Water also forms a <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical fluid</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_temperature" title="Critical temperature">critical temperature</a> is 647 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_pressure" title="Critical pressure">critical pressure</a> is 22.064 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">MPa</a>. In nature this only rarely occurs in extremely hostile conditions. A likely example of naturally occurring supercritical water is in the hottest parts of deep water <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vents" title="Hydrothermal vents">hydrothermal vents</a>, in which water is heated to the critical temperature by <a href="/wiki/Submarine_volcano" title="Submarine volcano">volcanic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plume_(hydrodynamics)" title="Plume (hydrodynamics)">plumes</a> and the critical pressure is caused by the weight of the ocean at the extreme depths where the vents are located. This pressure is reached at a depth of about 2200 meters: much less than the mean depth of the ocean (3800 meters).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat_capacity_and_heats_of_vaporization_and_fusion">Heat capacity and heats of vaporization and fusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Heat capacity and heats of vaporization and fusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heat_of_Vaporization_Water.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="508" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Heat_of_Vaporization_Water.png/220px-Heat_of_Vaporization_Water.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Heat_of_Vaporization_Water.png/330px-Heat_of_Vaporization_Water.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Heat_of_Vaporization_Water.png/440px-Heat_of_Vaporization_Water.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heat_of_Vaporization_Water.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heat of vaporization of water from melting to critical temperature</div></div></div>
<p>Water has a very high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat capacity</a> of 4.1814 J/(g·K) at 25 °C – the second highest among all the heteroatomic species (after <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>), as well as a high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_of_vaporization" title="Heat of vaporization">heat of vaporization</a> (40.65 kJ/mol or 2257 kJ/kg at the normal boiling point), both of which are a result of the extensive <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonding</a> between its molecules. These two unusual properties allow water to moderate Earth's <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> by buffering large fluctuations in temperature. Most of the additional energy stored in the climate system since 1970 has accumulated in the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The specific <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy_of_fusion" title="Enthalpy of fusion">enthalpy of fusion</a> (more commonly known as latent heat) of water is 333.55 kJ/kg at 0 °C: the same amount of energy is required to melt ice as to warm ice from −160 °C up to its melting point or to heat the same amount of water by about 80 °C. Of common substances, only that of ammonia is higher. This property confers resistance to melting on the ice of <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">drift ice</a>. Before and since the advent of mechanical <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration" title="Refrigeration">refrigeration</a>, ice was and still is in common use for retarding food spoilage.
</p><p>The specific heat capacity of ice at −10 °C is 2.03 J/(g·K)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide2003Chapter_6:_Properties_of_Ice_and_Supercooled_Water_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide2003Chapter_6:_Properties_of_Ice_and_Supercooled_Water-32">[29]</a></sup>
and the heat capacity of steam at 100 °C is 2.08 J/(g·K).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide20036._Properties_of_Water_and_Steam_as_a_Function_of_Temperature_and_Pressure_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide20036._Properties_of_Water_and_Steam_as_a_Function_of_Temperature_and_Pressure-33">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Density_of_water_and_ice">Density of water and ice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Density of water and ice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Density_of_ice_and_water_(en).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="573" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Density_of_ice_and_water_%28en%29.svg/220px-Density_of_ice_and_water_%28en%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Density_of_ice_and_water_%28en%29.svg/330px-Density_of_ice_and_water_%28en%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Density_of_ice_and_water_%28en%29.svg/440px-Density_of_ice_and_water_%28en%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Density_of_ice_and_water_(en).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Density of ice and water as a function of temperature</div></div></div><p>The <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of water is about 1 gram per cubic centimetre (62 lb/cu ft): this relationship was originally used to define the gram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-decree_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-decree-34">[31]</a></sup> The density varies with temperature, but not linearly: as the temperature increases, the density rises to a peak at  3.98 °C (39.16 °F) and then decreases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997625_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997625-35">[32]</a></sup> This unusual negative thermal expansion below 4 °C (39 °F) is also observed in molten <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> Regular, <a href="/wiki/Ice_Ih" title="Ice Ih">hexagonal ice</a> is also less dense than liquid water—upon freezing, the density of water decreases by about 9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>These effects are due to the reduction of thermal motion with cooling, which allows water molecules to form more hydrogen bonds that prevent the molecules from coming close to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997625_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997625-35">[32]</a></sup> While below 4 °C the breakage of hydrogen bonds due to heating allows water molecules to pack closer despite the increase in the thermal motion (which tends to expand a liquid),  above 4 °C water expands as the temperature increases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997625_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997625-35">[32]</a></sup> Water near the boiling point is about 4% less dense than water at 4 °C (39 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[d]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under increasing pressure, ice undergoes a number of transitions to other <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(materials_science)" title="Polymorphism (materials science)">polymorphs</a> with higher density than liquid water, such as <a href="/wiki/Ice_II" title="Ice II">ice II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_III" title="Ice III">ice III</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-density_amorphous_ice" title="High-density amorphous ice">high-density amorphous ice</a> (HDA), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Very-high-density_amorphous_ice" title="Very-high-density amorphous ice">very-high-density amorphous ice</a> (VHDA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997624_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997624-40">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anomalous_expansion_of_water_Summer_Winter.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="410" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Anomalous_expansion_of_water_Summer_Winter.svg/220px-Anomalous_expansion_of_water_Summer_Winter.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Anomalous_expansion_of_water_Summer_Winter.svg/330px-Anomalous_expansion_of_water_Summer_Winter.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Anomalous_expansion_of_water_Summer_Winter.svg/440px-Anomalous_expansion_of_water_Summer_Winter.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anomalous_expansion_of_water_Summer_Winter.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temperature distribution in a lake in summer and winter</div></div></div>
<p>The unusual density curve and lower density of ice than of water is vital to life—if water were most dense at the freezing point, then in winter the very cold water at the surface of lakes and other water bodies would sink, the lake could freeze from the bottom up, and all life in them would be killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[34]</a></sup> Furthermore, given that water is a good thermal insulator (due to its heat capacity), some frozen lakes might not completely thaw in summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[34]</a></sup> The layer of ice that floats on top insulates the water below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013493_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013493-41">[37]</a></sup> Water at about 4 °C (39 °F) also sinks to the bottom, thus keeping the temperature of the water at the bottom constant (see diagram).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Density_of_saltwater_and_ice">Density of saltwater and ice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Density of saltwater and ice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WOA09_sea-surf_DEN_AYool.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2217" data-file-width="3308" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/WOA09_sea-surf_DEN_AYool.png/310px-WOA09_sea-surf_DEN_AYool.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/WOA09_sea-surf_DEN_AYool.png/465px-WOA09_sea-surf_DEN_AYool.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/WOA09_sea-surf_DEN_AYool.png/620px-WOA09_sea-surf_DEN_AYool.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WOA09_sea-surf_DEN_AYool.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/World_Ocean_Atlas" title="World Ocean Atlas">WOA</a> surface density</div></div></div>
<p>The density of salt water depends on the dissolved salt content as well as the temperature. Ice still floats in the oceans, otherwise they would freeze from the bottom up. However, the salt content of oceans lowers the freezing point by about 1.9 °C<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-42">[38]</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Freezing-point_depression#Freezing-point_depression_of_a_solvent_and_a_solute" title="Freezing-point depression">here</a> for explanation) and lowers the temperature of the density maximum of water to the former freezing point at 0 °C. This is why, in ocean water, the downward convection of colder water is <i>not</i> blocked by an expansion of water as it becomes colder near the freezing point. The oceans' cold water near the freezing point continues to sink. So creatures that live at the bottom of cold oceans like the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a> generally live in water  4 °C colder than at the bottom of frozen-over <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> lakes and rivers.
</p><p>As the <a href="/wiki/Surface_science" title="Surface science">surface</a> of salt water begins to freeze (at −1.9 °C<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-42">[38]</a></sup> for normal salinity <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>, 3.5%) the ice that forms is essentially salt-free, with about the same density as freshwater ice. This ice floats on the surface, and the salt that is "frozen out" adds to the <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> and density of the sea water just below it, in a process known as <i><a href="/wiki/Brine_rejection" title="Brine rejection">brine rejection</a></i>. This denser salt water sinks by convection and the replacing seawater is subject to the same process. This produces essentially freshwater ice at −1.9 °C<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-42">[38]</a></sup> on the surface. The increased density of the sea water beneath the forming ice causes it to sink towards the bottom. On a large scale, the process of brine rejection and sinking cold salty water results in ocean currents forming to transport such water away from the Poles, leading to a global system of currents called the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscibility_and_condensation">Miscibility and condensation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Miscibility and condensation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relative_Humidity.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="481" data-file-width="500" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Relative_Humidity.png/220px-Relative_Humidity.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Relative_Humidity.png/330px-Relative_Humidity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Relative_Humidity.png/440px-Relative_Humidity.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relative_Humidity.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red line shows saturation</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">Humidity</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_water-miscible_solvents" title="List of water-miscible solvents">List of water-miscible solvents</a></div>
<p>Water is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miscible" title="Miscible">miscible</a> with many liquids, including <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> in all proportions. Water and most <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oils</a> are immiscible usually forming layers according to increasing density from the top. This can be predicted by comparing the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polarity</a>. Water being a relatively polar compound will tend to be miscible with liquids of high polarity such as ethanol and acetone, whereas compounds with low polarity will tend to be immiscible and poorly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soluble" title="Soluble">soluble</a> such as with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a>.
</p><p>As a gas, water vapor is completely miscible with air. On the other hand, the maximum water <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a> that is thermodynamically stable with the liquid (or solid) at a given temperature is relatively low compared with total atmospheric pressure.
For example, if the vapor's <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">partial pressure</a> is 2% of atmospheric pressure and the air is cooled from 25 °C, starting at about 22 °C water will start to condense, defining the <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">dew point</a>, and creating <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dew" title="Dew">dew</a>. The reverse process accounts for the fog burning off in the morning. If the humidity is increased at room temperature, for example, by running a hot shower or a bath, and the temperature stays about the same, the vapor soon reaches the pressure for phase change, and then condenses out as minute water droplets, commonly referred to as steam.
</p><p>A saturated gas or one with 100% relative humidity is when the vapor pressure of water in the air is at equilibrium with vapor pressure due to (liquid) water; water (or ice, if cool enough) will fail to lose mass through evaporation when exposed to saturated air. Because the amount of water vapor in air is small, relative humidity, the ratio of the partial pressure due to the water vapor to the saturated partial vapor pressure, is much more useful.
Vapor pressure above 100% relative humidity is called super-saturated and can occur if air is rapidly cooled, for example, by rising suddenly in an updraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[e]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vapor_pressure">Vapor pressure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vapor pressure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure_of_water" title="Vapor pressure of water">Vapor pressure of water</a></div>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vapor_Pressure_of_Water.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="1000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Vapor_Pressure_of_Water.png/440px-Vapor_Pressure_of_Water.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Vapor_Pressure_of_Water.png/660px-Vapor_Pressure_of_Water.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Vapor_Pressure_of_Water.png/880px-Vapor_Pressure_of_Water.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vapor_Pressure_of_Water.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vapor pressure diagrams of water</div></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Compressibility">Compressibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Compressibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Compressibility" title="Compressibility">compressibility</a> of water is a function of pressure and temperature. At 0 °C, at the limit of zero pressure, the compressibility is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990510000000000000♠</span>5.1<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−10</sup> Pa<sup>−1</sup></span>. At the zero-pressure limit, the compressibility reaches a minimum of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990440000000000000♠</span>4.4<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−10</sup> Pa<sup>−1</sup></span> around 45 °C before increasing again with increasing temperature. As the pressure is increased, the compressibility decreases, being <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990390000000000000♠</span>3.9<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−10</sup> Pa<sup>−1</sup></span> at 0 °C and 100 megapascals (1,000 bar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bulk_modulus" title="Bulk modulus">bulk modulus</a> of water is about 2.2 GPa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nave_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-nave-45">[40]</a></sup> The low compressibility of non-gases, and of water in particular, leads to their often being assumed as incompressible. The low compressibility of water means that even in the deep <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a> at 4 km depth, where pressures are 40 MPa, there is only a 1.8% decrease in volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nave_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-nave-45">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Triple_point">Triple point</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Triple point">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Triple_point#Triple_point_of_water" title="Triple point">Triple point § Triple point of water</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-left:12px">

<p>The <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> at which ordinary solid, liquid, and gaseous water coexist in equilibrium is a <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of water. This point has been used to define the base unit of temperature, the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a>, since 1954, and is thus set as having a temperature of 273.16 kelvin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20165_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20165-48">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="512" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg/440px-Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg/660px-Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg/880px-Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phase diagram of water</div></div></div><p> Due the existence of many <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(materials_science)" title="Polymorphism (materials science)">polymorphs</a> (forms) of ice, water has other triple points, which have either three polymorphs of ice or two polymorphs of ice and liquid in equilibrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20165_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20165-48">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Heinrich_Johann_Apollon_Tammann" title="Gustav Heinrich Johann Apollon Tammann">Gustav Heinrich Johann Apollon Tammann</a> in Göttingen produced data on several other triple points in the early 20th century. Kamb and others documented further triple points in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schleuter_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schleuter-49">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewisRice1922_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewisRice1922-51">[46]</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Melting_point">Melting point</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Melting point">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The melting point of ice is 0 °C (32 °F; 273 K) at standard pressure; however, pure liquid water can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supercooled" title="Supercooled">supercooled</a> well below that temperature without freezing if the liquid is not mechanically disturbed. It can remain in a fluid state down to its homogeneous <a href="/wiki/Nucleation" title="Nucleation">nucleation</a> point of about 231 K (−42 °C; −44 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup> The melting point of ordinary hexagonal ice falls slightly under moderately high pressures, by 0.0073 °C (0.0131 °F)/atm<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sharp_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-sharp-53">[f]</a></sup> or about 0.5 °C (0.90 °F)/70 atm<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sharp2_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-sharp2-54">[g]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharp198827_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharp198827-55">[48]</a></sup> as the stabilization energy of hydrogen bonding is exceeded by intermolecular repulsion, but as ice transforms into its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotropes" title="Allotropes">allotropes</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Ice#Phases" title="Ice">crystalline states of ice</a>) above 209.9 MPa (2,072 atm), the melting point increases markedly <a href="/wiki/Ice#At_different_pressures" title="Ice">with pressure</a>, i.e., reaching 355 K (82 °C) at 2.216 GPa (21,870 atm) (triple point of <a href="/wiki/Ice_VII" title="Ice VII">Ice VII</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAPWS_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAPWS-56">[49]</a></sup>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_properties">Electrical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Electrical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_conductivity">Electrical conductivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Electrical conductivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Pure water containing no exogenous <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> is an excellent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_insulation" title="Electrical insulation">insulator</a>, but not even "deionized" water is completely free of ions. Water undergoes <a href="/wiki/Self-ionization_of_water" title="Self-ionization of water">auto-ionization</a> in the liquid state, when two water molecules form one hydroxide anion (<span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) and one hydronium cation (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>).
</p><p>Because water is such a good solvent, it almost always has some <a href="/wiki/Solution" title="Solution">solute</a> dissolved in it, often a <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salt</a>. If water has even a tiny amount of such an impurity, then it can conduct electricity far  more readily.
</p><p>It is known that the theoretical maximum electrical resistivity for water is approximately 18.2 MΩ·cm (182 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilohm" title="Kilohm">kΩ</a>·m) at 25 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CondResist_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-CondResist-57">[50]</a></sup> This figure agrees well with what is typically seen on <a href="/wiki/Reverse_osmosis" title="Reverse osmosis">reverse osmosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultrafiltration" title="Ultrafiltration">ultra-filtered</a> and deionized ultra-pure water systems used, for instance, in semiconductor manufacturing plants. A salt or acid contaminant level exceeding even 100 parts per trillion (ppt) in otherwise ultra-pure water begins to noticeably lower its resistivity by up to several kΩ·m.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In pure water, sensitive equipment can detect a very slight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a> of 0.05501 ± 0.0001 <a href="/wiki/Siemens_(unit)" title="Siemens (unit)">µS</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimeter" title="Centimeter">cm</a> at 25.00 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CondResist_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-CondResist-57">[50]</a></sup> Water can also be <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolyzed</a> into oxygen and hydrogen gases but in the absence of dissolved ions this is a very slow process, as very little current is conducted. In ice, the primary charge carriers are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protons" title="Protons">protons</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Proton_conductor" title="Proton conductor">proton conductor</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[51]</a></sup> Ice was previously thought to have a small but measurable conductivity of 1<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>−<span style="white-space:nowrap">10</span></sup> S/cm, but this conductivity is now thought to be almost entirely from surface defects, and without those, ice is an insulator with an immeasurably small conductivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997625_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997625-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarity_and_hydrogen_bonding">Polarity and hydrogen bonding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Polarity and hydrogen bonding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">Chemical polarity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H2O_Polarization_V.1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="101" data-file-width="149" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/H2O_Polarization_V.1.svg/220px-H2O_Polarization_V.1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/H2O_Polarization_V.1.svg/330px-H2O_Polarization_V.1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/H2O_Polarization_V.1.svg/440px-H2O_Polarization_V.1.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H2O_Polarization_V.1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diagram showing the partial charges on the atoms in a water molecule</div></div></div>
<p>An important feature of water is its polar nature. The structure has a <a href="/wiki/Bent_molecular_geometry" title="Bent molecular geometry">bent molecular geometry</a> for the two hydrogens from the oxygen vertex. The oxygen atom also has two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lone_pairs" title="Lone pairs">lone pairs</a> of electrons. One effect usually ascribed to the lone pairs is that the H–O–H gas phase bend angle is 104.48°,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[52]</a></sup> which is smaller than the typical <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedral_molecular_geometry" title="Tetrahedral molecular geometry">tetrahedral</a> angle of 109.47°. The lone pairs are closer to the oxygen atom than the electrons <a href="/wiki/Sigma_bond" title="Sigma bond">sigma bonded</a> to the hydrogens, so they require more space. The increased repulsion of the lone pairs forces the O–H bonds closer to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013393_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013393-60">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another consequence of its <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a> is that water is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule">polar molecule</a>. Due to the difference in <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Bond_dipole_moment" title="Bond dipole moment">bond dipole moment</a> points from each H to the O, making the oxygen partially negative and each hydrogen partially positive. A large molecular <a href="/wiki/Electric_dipole_moment" title="Electric dipole moment">dipole</a>, points from a region between the two hydrogen atoms to the oxygen atom. The charge differences cause water molecules to aggregate (the relatively positive areas being attracted to the relatively negative areas). This attraction, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonding</a>, explains many of the properties of water, such as its solvent properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbellFarrell200737–38_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbellFarrell200737–38-61">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although hydrogen bonding is a relatively weak attraction compared to the covalent bonds within the water molecule itself, it is responsible for a number of water's physical properties. These properties include its relatively high <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting</a> and boiling point temperatures: more energy is required to break the hydrogen bonds between water molecules. In contrast, hydrogen sulfide (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span>), has much weaker hydrogen bonding due to sulfur's lower electronegativity. <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span> is a gas at <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a>, in spite of hydrogen sulfide having nearly twice the molar mass of water. The extra bonding between water molecules also gives liquid water a large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat capacity</a>. This high heat capacity makes water a good heat storage medium (coolant) and heat shield.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cohesion_and_adhesion">Cohesion and adhesion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cohesion and adhesion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spider_web_Luc_Viatour.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2373" data-file-width="3160" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Spider_web_Luc_Viatour.jpg/220px-Spider_web_Luc_Viatour.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Spider_web_Luc_Viatour.jpg/330px-Spider_web_Luc_Viatour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Spider_web_Luc_Viatour.jpg/440px-Spider_web_Luc_Viatour.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spider_web_Luc_Viatour.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dew" title="Dew">Dew</a> drops adhering to a <a href="/wiki/Spider_web" title="Spider web">spider web</a></div></div></div>
<p>Water molecules stay close to each other (<a href="/wiki/Cohesion_(chemistry)" title="Cohesion (chemistry)">cohesion</a>), due to the collective action of hydrogen bonds between water molecules. These hydrogen bonds are constantly breaking, with new bonds being formed with different water molecules; but at any given time in a sample of liquid water, a large portion of the molecules are held together by such bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbellReece200947_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbellReece200947-62">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water also has high <a href="/wiki/Adhesion" title="Adhesion">adhesion</a> properties because of its polar nature. On extremely clean/smooth <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> the water may form a thin film because the molecular forces between glass and water molecules (adhesive forces) are stronger than the cohesive forces.
In biological cells and <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a>, water is in contact with membrane and protein surfaces that are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophilic" title="Hydrophilic">hydrophilic</a>; that is, surfaces that have a strong attraction to water. <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">Irving Langmuir</a> observed a strong repulsive force between hydrophilic surfaces. To dehydrate hydrophilic surfaces—to remove the strongly held layers of water of hydration—requires doing substantial work against these forces, called hydration forces. These forces are very large but decrease rapidly over a nanometer or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[56]</a></sup> They are important in biology, particularly when cells are dehydrated by exposure to dry atmospheres or to extracellular freezing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_tension">Surface tension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Surface tension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paper_Clip_Surface_Tension_1_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1369" data-file-width="1137" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Paper_Clip_Surface_Tension_1_crop.jpg/220px-Paper_Clip_Surface_Tension_1_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Paper_Clip_Surface_Tension_1_crop.jpg/330px-Paper_Clip_Surface_Tension_1_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Paper_Clip_Surface_Tension_1_crop.jpg/440px-Paper_Clip_Surface_Tension_1_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paper_Clip_Surface_Tension_1_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This <a href="/wiki/Paper_clip" title="Paper clip">paper clip</a> is under the water level, which has risen gently and smoothly. Surface tension prevents the clip from submerging and the water from overflowing the glass edges.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temperature_dependence_surface_tension_of_water.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="421" data-file-width="589" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Temperature_dependence_surface_tension_of_water.svg/220px-Temperature_dependence_surface_tension_of_water.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Temperature_dependence_surface_tension_of_water.svg/330px-Temperature_dependence_surface_tension_of_water.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Temperature_dependence_surface_tension_of_water.svg/440px-Temperature_dependence_surface_tension_of_water.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temperature_dependence_surface_tension_of_water.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Temperature dependence of the surface tension of pure water</div></div></div>
<p>Water has an unusually high <a href="/wiki/Surface_tension" title="Surface tension">surface tension</a> of 71.99 mN/m at 25 °C<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide2003Surface_Tension_of_Common_Liquids_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide2003Surface_Tension_of_Common_Liquids-65">[58]</a></sup> which is caused by the strength of the hydrogen bonding between water molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201346_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201346-66">[59]</a></sup> This allows insects to walk on water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201346_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201346-66">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Capillary_action">Capillary action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Capillary action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Because water has strong cohesive and adhesive forces, it exhibits capillary action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013458–459_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013458–459-67">[60]</a></sup> Strong cohesion from hydrogen bonding and adhesion allows trees to transport water more than 100 m upward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201346_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReece_et_al.201346-66">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_as_a_solvent">Water as a solvent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Water as a solvent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aqueous_solution" title="Aqueous solution">Aqueous solution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Havasu_Falls_2_md.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1231" data-file-width="821" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Havasu_Falls_2_md.jpg/170px-Havasu_Falls_2_md.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Havasu_Falls_2_md.jpg/255px-Havasu_Falls_2_md.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Havasu_Falls_2_md.jpg/340px-Havasu_Falls_2_md.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Havasu_Falls_2_md.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Presence of <a href="/wiki/Colloid" title="Colloid">colloidal</a> <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> from high concentrations of dissolved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lime_(mineral)" title="Lime (mineral)">lime</a> turns the water of <a href="/wiki/Havasu_Falls" title="Havasu Falls">Havasu Falls</a> turquoise.</div></div></div>
<p>Water is an excellent <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> due to its high dielectric constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997627_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997627-68">[61]</a></sup> Substances that mix well and dissolve in water are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophilic" title="Hydrophilic">hydrophilic</a> ("water-loving") substances, while those that do not mix well with water are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> ("water-fearing") substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013518_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013518-69">[62]</a></sup> The ability of a substance to dissolve in water is determined by whether or not the substance can match or better the strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipole-dipole_interaction" title="Dipole-dipole interaction">attractive forces</a> that water molecules generate between other water molecules. If a substance has properties that do not allow it to overcome these strong intermolecular forces, the molecules are <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitated out</a> from the water. Contrary to the common misconception, water and hydrophobic substances do not "repel", and the hydration of a hydrophobic surface is energetically, but not entropically, favorable.
</p><p>When an ionic or polar compound enters water, it is surrounded by water molecules (<a href="/wiki/Solvation" title="Solvation">hydration</a>). The relatively small size of water molecules (~ 3 angstroms) allows many water molecules to surround one molecule of <a href="/wiki/Solution" title="Solution">solute</a>. The partially negative dipole ends of the water are attracted to positively charged components of the solute, and vice versa for the positive dipole ends.
</p><p>In general, ionic and polar substances such as <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> are relatively soluble in water, and non-polar substances such as fats and oils are not. Non-polar molecules stay together in water because it is energetically more favorable for the water molecules to hydrogen bond to each other than to engage in <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force" title="Van der Waals force">van der Waals interactions</a> with non-polar molecules.
</p><p>An example of an ionic solute is <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">table salt</a>; the sodium chloride, NaCl, separates into <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cations</a> and <span class="chemf nowrap">Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anions</a>, each being surrounded by water molecules. The ions are then easily transported away from their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystal_lattice" title="Crystal lattice">crystalline lattice</a> into solution. An example of a nonionic solute is <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">table sugar</a>. The water dipoles make hydrogen bonds with the polar regions of the sugar molecule (OH groups) and allow it to be carried away into solution.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_tunneling">Quantum tunneling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Quantum tunneling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quantum_tunneling_of_water" title="Quantum tunneling of water">Quantum tunneling of water</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_tunneling" title="Quantum tunneling">quantum tunneling</a> dynamics in water was reported as early as 1992. At that time it was known that there are motions which destroy and regenerate the weak <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bond</a> by internal rotations of the substituent water <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monomers" title="Monomers">monomers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[63]</a></sup> On 18 March 2016, it was reported that the hydrogen bond can be broken by quantum tunneling in the water <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexamer" title="Hexamer">hexamer</a>. Unlike previously reported tunneling motions in water, this involved the concerted breaking of two hydrogen bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[64]</a></sup> Later in the same year, the discovery of the quantum tunneling of water molecules was reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic_absorption">Electromagnetic absorption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Electromagnetic absorption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_absorption_by_water" title="Electromagnetic absorption by water">Electromagnetic absorption by water</a></div>
<p>Water is relatively transparent to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_ultraviolet" title="Near ultraviolet">near ultraviolet</a> light, and <a href="/wiki/Far-red" title="Far-red">far-red</a> light, but it absorbs most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_light" title="Ultraviolet light">ultraviolet light</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_light" title="Infrared light">infrared light</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwaves</a>. Most <a href="/wiki/Photoreceptor_protein" title="Photoreceptor protein">photoreceptors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_pigment" title="Photosynthetic pigment">photosynthetic pigments</a> utilize the portion of the light spectrum that is transmitted well through water. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microwave_ovens" title="Microwave ovens">Microwave ovens</a> take advantage of water's opacity to microwave radiation to heat the water inside of foods. Water's light blue colour is caused by weak <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">absorption</a> in the red part of the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braun-73">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="400" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg/220px-3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg/330px-3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg/440px-3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> (1) between molecules of water</div></div></div>
<p>A single water molecule can participate in a maximum of four <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> because it can accept two bonds using the lone pairs on oxygen and donate two hydrogen atoms. Other molecules like <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fluoride" title="Hydrogen fluoride">hydrogen fluoride</a>, ammonia and <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> can also form hydrogen bonds. However, they do not show anomalous <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kinematics" title="Kinematics">kinetic</a> or structural properties like those observed in water because none of them can form four hydrogen bonds: either they cannot donate or accept hydrogen atoms, or there are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steric" title="Steric">steric</a> effects in bulky residues. In water, intermolecular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrahedral" title="Tetrahedral">tetrahedral</a> structures form due to the four hydrogen bonds, thereby forming an open structure and a three-dimensional bonding network, resulting in the anomalous decrease in density when cooled below 4 °C. This repeated, constantly reorganizing unit defines a three-dimensional network extending throughout the liquid. This view is based upon neutron scattering studies and computer simulations, and it makes sense in the light of the unambiguously tetrahedral arrangement of water molecules in ice structures.
</p><p>However, there is an alternative theory for the structure of water. In 2004, a controversial paper from <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_University" title="Stockholm University">Stockholm University</a> suggested that water molecules in liquid form typically bind not to four but to only two others; thus forming chains and rings. The term "string theory of water" (which is not to be confused with the <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a> of physics) was coined. These observations were based upon X-ray absorption spectroscopy that probed the local environment of individual oxygen atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>At standard conditions, water is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule">polar</a> liquid that slightly dissociates <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionately</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self_ionization_of_water" title="Self ionization of water">self ionizes</a> into an <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a> ion and <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> ion.
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_constant" title="Dissociation constant">dissociation constant</a> for this dissociation is commonly symbolized as <i>K<sub>w</sub></i> and has a value of about 10<sup>−<span style="white-space:nowrap">14</span></sup> at 25 °C; see <a href="/wiki/Water_(data_page)#Self_ionization" title="Water (data page)">here</a> for values at other temperatures. Pure water has a concentration of the <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxide</a> ion (<span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) equal to that of the hydrogen ion (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>), which gives a <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of 7 at 25 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyd2000105_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyd2000105-76">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geochemistry">Geochemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Geochemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Action of water on rock over long periods of time typically leads to <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_erosion" title="Water erosion">water erosion</a>, physical processes that convert solid rocks and minerals into soil and sediment, but under some conditions chemical reactions with water occur as well, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Metasomatism" title="Metasomatism">metasomatism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mineral_hydration" title="Mineral hydration">mineral hydration</a>, a type of chemical alteration of a rock which produces <a href="/wiki/Clay_minerals" title="Clay minerals">clay minerals</a>. It also occurs when <a href="/wiki/Portland_cement" title="Portland cement">Portland cement</a> hardens.
</p><p>Water ice can form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrate_compounds" title="Clathrate compounds">clathrate compounds</a>, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrate_hydrates" title="Clathrate hydrates">clathrate hydrates</a>, with a variety of small molecules that can be embedded in its spacious crystal lattice. The most notable of these is <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">methane clathrate</a>, 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>·23H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>, naturally found in large quantities on the ocean floor.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acidity_in_nature">Acidity in nature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Acidity in nature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rain is generally mildly acidic, with a pH between 5.2 and 5.8 if not having any acid stronger than carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoyd2000106_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoyd2000106-77">[70]</a></sup> If high amounts of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> oxides are present in the air, they too will dissolve into the cloud and rain drops, producing <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopologues">Isotopologues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Isotopologues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Several <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of both hydrogen and oxygen exist, giving rise to several known <a href="/wiki/Isotopologue" title="Isotopologue">isotopologues</a> of water. <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Standard_Mean_Ocean_Water" title="Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water">Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water</a> is the current international standard for water isotopes. Naturally occurring water is almost completely composed of the neutron-less hydrogen isotope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-1" title="Hydrogen-1">protium</a>. Only 155 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> include <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span> or D), a hydrogen isotope with one neutron, and fewer than 20 parts per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quintillion" title="Quintillion">quintillion</a> include <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H</span> or T), which has two neutrons. Oxygen also has three stable isotopes, with <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">16</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>O</span> present in 99.76%, <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">17</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>O</span> in 0.04%, and <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">18</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>O</span> in 0.2% of water molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[71]</a></sup>
