</p><p>Deuterium oxide, <span class="chemf nowrap">D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>, is also known as <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a> because of its higher density. It is used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a>. Tritium is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a>, decaying with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 4500 days; <span class="chemf nowrap">THO</span> exists in nature only in minute quantities, being produced primarily via cosmic ray-induced nuclear reactions in the atmosphere. Water with one protium and one deuterium atom <span class="chemf nowrap">HDO</span> occurs naturally in ordinary water in low concentrations (~0.03%) and <span class="chemf nowrap">D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> in far lower amounts (0.000003%) and any such molecules are temporary as the atoms recombine.
</p><p>The most notable physical differences between <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>, other than the simple difference in specific mass, involve properties that are affected by hydrogen bonding, such as freezing and boiling, and other kinetic effects. This is because the nucleus of deuterium is twice as heavy as protium, and this causes noticeable differences in bonding energies. The difference in boiling points allows the isotopologues to be separated. The <a href="/wiki/Self-diffusion" title="Self-diffusion">self-diffusion</a> coefficient of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> at 25 °C is 23% higher than the value of <span class="chemf nowrap">D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Isotopeneffekt_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Isotopeneffekt-79">[72]</a></sup> Because water molecules exchange hydrogen atoms with one another, hydrogen deuterium oxide (DOH) is much more common in low-purity heavy water than pure dideuterium monoxide <span class="chemf nowrap">D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.
</p><p>Consumption of pure isolated <span class="chemf nowrap">D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> may affect biochemical processes – ingestion of large amounts impairs kidney and central nervous system function. Small quantities can be consumed without any ill-effects; humans are generally unaware of taste differences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sci35_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sci35-80">[73]</a></sup> but sometimes report a burning sensation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopSci35_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopSci35-81">[74]</a></sup> or sweet flavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopSci37_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopSci37-82">[75]</a></sup> Very large amounts of heavy water must be consumed for any toxicity to become apparent. Rats, however, are able to avoid heavy water by smell, and it is toxic to many animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PnB_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-PnB-83">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Deuterium-depleted_water" title="Deuterium-depleted water"><i>Light water</i></a> refers to deuterium-depleted water (DDW), water in which the deuterium content has been reduced below the standard <span class="nowrap">155 ppm</span> level.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It is the most abundant substance on Earth and also the third most abundant molecule in the universe, after <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">CO</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20162_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.20162-23">[21]</a></sup> 0.23 ppm of the earth's mass is water and 97.39% of the global water volume of 1.38<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">9</span></sup> km<sup>3</sup> is found in the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.201629_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeingärtner_et_al.201629-84">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions">Reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acid-base_reactions">Acid-base reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Acid-base reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Water is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoteric" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a>: it has the ability to act as either an <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">base</a> in chemical reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013659_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013659-85">[78]</a></sup> According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted-Lowry" title="Brønsted-Lowry">Brønsted-Lowry</a> definition, an acid is a proton (<span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Hydron_(chemistry)" title="Hydron (chemistry)">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span>) donor and a base is a proton acceptor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013654_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013654-86">[79]</a></sup> When reacting with a stronger acid, water acts as a base; when reacting with a stronger base, it acts as an acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013654_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013654-86">[79]</a></sup> For instance, water receives an <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ion from HCl when <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> is formed:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">HCl</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(acid)</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(base)</span></span></span></span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>In the reaction with <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, water donates a <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> ion, and is thus acting as an acid:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(base)</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(acid)</span></span></span></span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Because the oxygen atom in water has two <a href="/wiki/Lone_pair" title="Lone pair">lone pairs</a>, water often acts as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_base" title="Lewis base">Lewis base</a>, or electron pair donor, in reactions with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acids</a>, although it can also react with Lewis bases, forming hydrogen bonds between the electron pair donors and the hydrogen atoms of water. <a href="/wiki/HSAB_theory" title="HSAB theory">HSAB theory</a> describes water as both a weak hard acid and a weak hard base, meaning that it reacts preferentially with other hard species:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(Lewis acid)</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(Lewis base)</span></span></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.5em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="chemf nowrap">Fe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(Lewis acid)</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(Lewis base)</span></span></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<dl><dd><span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="chemf nowrap">Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(Lewis base)</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>(Lewis acid)</span></span></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Cl(<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>When a salt of a weak acid or of a weak base is dissolved in water, water can partially <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolyze</a> the salt, producing the corresponding base or acid, which gives aqueous solutions of <a href="/wiki/Soap" title="Soap">soap</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baking_soda" title="Baking soda">baking soda</a> their basic pH:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> ⇌ NaOH + <span class="chemf nowrap">NaHCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ligand_chemistry">Ligand chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ligand chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Water's Lewis base character makes it a common <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> in <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> complexes, examples of which include <a href="/wiki/Metal_aquo_complex" title="Metal aquo complex">metal aquo complexes</a> such as <span class="chemf nowrap">Fe(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> to <a href="/wiki/Perrhenic_acid" title="Perrhenic acid">perrhenic acid</a>, which contains two water molecules coordinated to a <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a> center.  In solid <a href="/wiki/Water_of_crystallization" title="Water of crystallization">hydrates</a>,  water can be either a ligand or simply lodged in the framework, or both.  Thus, <span class="chemf nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrous_sulfate" title="Ferrous sulfate">FeSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>·7H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</a></span> consists of [Fe<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> centers and one "lattice water". Water is typically a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monodentate" title="Monodentate">monodentate</a> ligand, i.e., it forms only one bond with the central atom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013984_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013984-87">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:344px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H-bondingFeSO47aq.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="605" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/H-bondingFeSO47aq.tif/lossless-page1-342px-H-bondingFeSO47aq.tif.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/H-bondingFeSO47aq.tif/lossless-page1-513px-H-bondingFeSO47aq.tif.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/H-bondingFeSO47aq.tif/lossless-page1-684px-H-bondingFeSO47aq.tif.png 2x" width="342"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H-bondingFeSO47aq.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some hydrogen-bonding contacts in FeSO<sub>4</sub><sup>.</sup>7H<sub>2</sub>O. This <a href="/wiki/Metal_aquo_complex" title="Metal aquo complex">metal aquo complex</a> crystallizes with one molecule of "lattice" water, which interacts with the sulfate and with the [Fe(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> centers.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_chemistry">Organic chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Organic chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a hard base, water reacts readily with organic <a href="/wiki/Carbocation" title="Carbocation">carbocations</a>; for example in an <a href="/wiki/Hydration_reaction" title="Hydration reaction">hydration reaction</a>, a hydroxyl group (<span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) and an acidic proton are added to the two carbon atoms bonded together in the carbon-carbon double bond, resulting in an alcohol. When addition of water to an organic molecule cleaves the molecule in two, <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> is said to occur. Notable examples of hydrolysis are the <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">saponification</a> of fats and the <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a> of proteins and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polysaccharides" title="Polysaccharides">polysaccharides</a>. Water can also be a <a href="/wiki/Leaving_group" title="Leaving group">leaving group</a> in <a href="/wiki/SN2_reaction" title="SN2 reaction">S<sub>N</sub>2 substitution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elimination_reaction" title="Elimination reaction">E2 elimination</a> reactions; the latter is then known as a <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydration reaction</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_in_redox_reactions">Water in redox reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Water in redox reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Water contains hydrogen in the <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> +1 and oxygen in the oxidation state −2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013171_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013171-88">[81]</a></sup> It oxidizes chemicals such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrides" title="Hydrides">hydrides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali</a> metals, and some <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth</a> metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013932,_936_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013932,_936-90">[83]</a></sup> One example of an alkali metal reacting with water is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013338_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013338-91">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Na + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Some other reactive metals, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminum" title="Aluminum">aluminum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>, are oxidized by water as well, but their oxides adhere to the metal and form a <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passive</a> protective layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013862_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013862-92">[85]</a></sup> Note that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rusting" title="Rusting">rusting</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> is a reaction between iron and oxygen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013981_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013981-93">[86]</a></sup> that is dissolved in water, not between iron and water.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Heterogeneous_water_oxidation" title="Heterogeneous water oxidation">Water can be oxidized</a> to emit oxygen gas, but very few oxidants react with water even if their reduction potential is greater than the potential of <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>/H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>. Almost all such reactions require a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECharlot2007275_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECharlot2007275-94">[87]</a></sup>
An example of the oxidation of water is:
</p>
<dl><dd>4 <span class="chemf nowrap">AgF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → 4 AgF + 4 HF + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrolysis">Electrolysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Electrolysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">Electrolysis of water</a></div>
<p>Water can be split into its constituent elements, hydrogen and oxygen, by passing an electric current through it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013866_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013866-95">[88]</a></sup> This process is called electrolysis.
The cathode half reaction is:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 2 <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The anode  half reaction is:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 4 <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The gases produced bubble to the surface, where they can be collected. The required potential for the electrolysis of pure water is 1.23 V at 25 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013866_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZumdahlZumdahl2013866-95">[88]</a></sup> The operating potential is actually 1.48 V or higher in practical electrolysis.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Henry_Cavendish" title="Henry Cavendish">Henry Cavendish</a> showed that water was composed of oxygen and hydrogen in 1781.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997601_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997601-96">[89]</a></sup> The first decomposition of water into hydrogen and oxygen, by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a>, was done in 1800 by English chemist <a href="/wiki/William_Nicholson_(chemist)" title="William Nicholson (chemist)">William Nicholson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Carlisle" title="Anthony Carlisle">Anthony Carlisle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997601_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenwoodEarnshaw1997601-96">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[90]</a></sup> In 1805, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac">Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a> showed that water is composed of two parts hydrogen and one part oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilbert_Newton_Lewis" title="Gilbert Newton Lewis">Gilbert Newton Lewis</a> isolated the first sample of pure <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a> in 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The properties of water have historically been used to define various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperature_conversion" title="Temperature conversion">temperature scales</a>. Notably, the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">Kelvin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">Celsius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rankine_scale" title="Rankine scale">Rankine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">Fahrenheit</a> scales were, or currently are, defined by the freezing and boiling points of water. The less common scales of <a href="/wiki/Delisle_scale" title="Delisle scale">Delisle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newton_scale" title="Newton scale">Newton</a>, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9aumur_scale" title="Réaumur scale">Réaumur</a> and <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B8mer_scale" title="Rømer scale">Rømer</a> were defined similarly. The <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of water is a more commonly used standard point today.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Properties_of_water&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The accepted <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature_of_inorganic_chemistry" title="IUPAC nomenclature of inorganic chemistry">IUPAC</a> name of water is <i>oxidane</i> or simply <i>water</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeighFavreMetanomski199834_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeighFavreMetanomski199834-100">[93]</a></sup> or its equivalent in different languages, although there are other systematic names which can be used to describe the molecule. Oxidane is only intended to be used as the name of the mononuclear <a href="/wiki/Parent_hydride" title="Parent hydride">parent hydride</a> used for naming derivatives of water by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substitutive_nomenclature" title="Substitutive nomenclature">substituent nomenclature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIUPAC200585_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIUPAC200585-101">[94]</a></sup> These derivatives commonly have other recommended names. For example, the name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> is recommended over <i>oxidanyl</i> for the –OH group. The name oxane is explicitly mentioned by the IUPAC as being unsuitable for this purpose, since it is already the name of a cyclic ether also known as <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydropyran" title="Tetrahydropyran">tetrahydropyran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeighFavreMetanomski199899_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeighFavreMetanomski199899-102">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The simplest systematic name of water is <i>hydrogen oxide</i>. This is analogous to related compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterium_oxide" title="Deuterium oxide">deuterium oxide</a> (heavy water). Using chemical nomenclature for <a href="/wiki/Chemical_nomenclature#Type-I_ionic_binary_compounds" title="Chemical nomenclature">type I ionic binary compounds</a>, water would take the name <i>hydrogen monoxide</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeighFavreMetanomski199827–28_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeighFavreMetanomski199827–28-104">[97]</a></sup> but this is not among the names published by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeighFavreMetanomski199834_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeighFavreMetanomski199834-100">[93]</a></sup> Another name is <i>dihydrogen monoxide</i>, which is a rarely used name of water, and mostly used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihydrogen_monoxide_hoax" title="Dihydrogen monoxide hoax">dihydrogen monoxide hoax</a>.
</p><p>Other systematic names for water include <i>hydroxic acid</i>, <i>hydroxylic acid</i>, and <i>hydrogen hydroxide</i>, using acid and base names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[h]</a></sup> None of these exotic names are used widely. The polarized form of the water molecule, <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, is also called <a href="/wiki/Hydron_(chemistry)" title="Hydron (chemistry)">hydron</a> hydroxide by IUPAC nomenclature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Water substance</i> is a term used for hydrogen oxide (H<sub>2</sub>O) when one does not wish to specify whether one is speaking of liquid <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a>, some form of <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>, or a component in a mixture or mineral.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation report">Rhein, M.; Rintoul, S.R. IPCC WGI AR5 (Report). p. 257. <q>Ocean warming dominates the global energy change inventory.  Warming of the ocean accounts for about 93% of the increase in the Earth's energy inventory between 1971 and 2010 (high confidence), with warming of the upper (0 to 700 m) ocean accounting for about 64% of the total. Melting ice (including Arctic sea ice, ice sheets and glaciers) and warming of the continents and atmosphere account for the remainder of the change in energy.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=report&amp;rft.btitle=IPCC+WGI+AR5&amp;rft.pages=257&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.aulast=Rhein&amp;rft.aufirst=M.&amp;rft.au=Rintoul%2C+S.R.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.climatechange2013.org%2Fimages%2Freport%2FWG1AR5_Chapter03_FINAL.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AProperties+of+water"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
